    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="031bc753c460443484ae68eb61d5735b">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'eb0c1788-d54c-406b-b202-2e5332d14793'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

            }

                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)

                     <p class="author-name">Xiaobo Ji</p>
                     <p>College of Chemistry and Chemical Engineering, Central South University, Changsha, 410083 China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Ji/Xiaobo">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>
         </div>
      </div>
   </div>

and Strengthened SEI Films link" href="https://doi.org/10.1002/adfm.202413488">https://doi.org/10.1002/adfm.202413488</a></div><div class="epub-section cited-by-count"><span>Citations: <a    <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d68492822" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium metal batteries with high theoretical energy density and low redox potential possess vast application prospects. However, the inevitable side reactions between Na metal anode and electrolyte, the formation of Na dendrite during cycling, and the uneven electric field distribution at the interface will lead to a decrease in the cycling stability of the battery. Herein, N and S co-doped carbon dots are employed as electrolyte additives in view of their quantum size, excellent dispersion, and polar surface functional groups. It is found that the quantum-sized carbon core and sodiophilic surface functional groups of N,S-CDs enable the homogenous deposition of Na<sup>+</sup> and the involvement of N,S-CDs in the formation of solid electrolyte film. The N,S-CDs co-deposition guarantees the uniform sodium plating in the initial cycle, avoiding the routine dendrite growth. The strength and stability of SEI are boosted with the assistance of inorganics such as Na<sub>3</sub>N and Na<sub>2</sub>S, which accelerate the transport of Na<sup>+</sup> flux and realize rapid charge transfer at the interface, ensuring high reversibility of sodium ion plating/stripping. Na||Cu half-cells achieve a coulombic efficiency of 99% after 250 cycles under 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−1</sup>, and Na||Na symmetric cells continuously cycle over 1200&nbsp;h at a current density of 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202413488-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202413488-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Rechargeable lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been rapidly expanded due to their advantages of high energy density, long cycle life, and low self-discharge.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0001_R_d68492806e381" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. IJEM.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> LIBs have been widely used in portable electronic devices and electric vehicles,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0002_R_d68492806e386" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - J. Energy Chem.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> yet the limited lithium resources and the constantly rising prices of lithium battery-related resources limit their application in new energy storage devices.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0003_R_d68492806e391" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Nano">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium-ion battery (SIB) is considered a replacement for LIB in some fields, such as large-scale energy storage and low-speed electric vehicles, because of its wide distribution and low cost of sodium resources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0004_R_d68492806e397" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Funct. Mater. SmartMat">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The increasing demand for high energy density and low-cost battery systems has enabled metal battery a research hotspot.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0005_R_d68492806e402" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Carbon Energy Prog. Nat. Sci. Mater. Int.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium metal has a high theoretical specific capacity (1166&nbsp;mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and a low redox potential (−2.71&nbsp;V compared to a standard hydrogen electrode), undoubtedly making it an ideal anode for sodium-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0006_R_d68492806e410" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Chem. Eng. J.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, sodium with high reactivity undergoes side reactions with electrolytes to generate solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film, which consumes the active electrode and electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0007_R_d68492806e415" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Chin. Chem. Lett.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The uneven deposition of sodium ions will induce protrusions during the plating/stripping process. Also, the tremendous volume expansion of Na metal is adverse to SEI stabilization, leading to the growth of dendrite and ultimately piercing the separator to post safety hazards.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0008_R_d68492806e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem.A Eur. J.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, achieving uniform deposition of sodium ions to regulate the growth of sodium dendrite is crucial for sodium metal batteries (SMBs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0009_R_d68492806e426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Mater. Today Energy">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Strategies such as improving the metal anode structure,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0010_R_d68492806e431" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> optimizing the electrolyte system,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0011_R_d68492806e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nano Lett.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> constructing artificial SEI film, and separator modification have been proposed to regulate the growth of dendrite.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0012_R_d68492806e442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nano Lett.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Researchers have found that carbon dots with quantum size, large specific surface area, abundant surface functional groups, and active sites hold great promise for enhancing the cycling stability of metal batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0013_R_d68492806e449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Nano">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Carbon dots are less than 10&nbsp;nm in 3D, with structures containing both a carbonaceous core and a surface shell layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0014_R_d68492806e454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a highly promising carbon nanomaterial, carbon dots are widely used in electrochemical energy storage due to their properties including excellent dispersibility and adjustable surface functional groups.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0015_R_d68492806e459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - WIREs Nanomedicine and Nanobiotechnology">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The conjugated graphitized structure of carbon dots offers their rapid electron transport capability, making them excellent electron donors/acceptors and thus performing outstandingly in electrochemical reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0016_R_d68492806e465" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Appl. Electron. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The abundant functional groups on the surface of carbon dots allow them to bind other metal ions or anchor metal nanoparticles, preventing them from agglomerating.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0017_R_d68492806e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Energy Chem.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Numerous adjustable active sites on the surface of heteroatom-doped carbon dots enhance the binding ability with metal ions and thus improve the diffusion kinetics of metal ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0018_R_d68492806e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Small-sized carbon dots with polar functional groups are extremely well dispersed in solvents and can be used as electrolyte additives. Previous studies have demonstrated the effect of carbon dots as electrolyte additives on inducing uniform deposition of Li<sup>+</sup>/Zn<sup>2+</sup>, effectively inhibiting the growth of Li/Zn dendrite.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0019_R_d68492806e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However the detailed effect mechanism of carbon dots in the electrochemical process is insufficient.</p>
         
         <p>Inspired by this, N,S co-doped carbon dots (N,S-CDs) were synthesized and employed as electrolyte additives for SMBs to regulate the transport behavior of Na<sup>+</sup>, thereby achieving the ultra-stale cycling performance of SMBs. The diffusion, deposition, and reaction process were studied in detail. DFT calculations demonstrate that the rich functional groups on the surface of N,S-CDs endow them with stronger binding energy to Na<sup>+</sup>. Motivated by electric field force and solution convection, a quantum-sized carbon core carries Na<sup>+</sup> to the surface of the collector. Confocal fluorescence microscopy evidenced that carbon dots and Na<sup>+</sup> co-deposited on the surface of the collector under electric field force and ion diffusion. Some carbon dots contributed to the formation of SEI with inner layers enriched with inorganic components such as NaF, Na<sub>3</sub>N, Na<sub>2</sub>S, etc. NaF guarantees better mechanical strength and surface interface properties of SEI, enhancing the strength and stability of SEI, and consequently inhibiting the side reactions of sodium and solvent molecules. Additionally, the low diffusion energy barrier of Na<sup>+</sup> in Na<sub>3</sub>N and Na<sub>2</sub>S enables its rapid charge transfer at the electrode-electrolyte interface and ensures the initial uniform deposition of Na<sup>+</sup>. Part of the carbon dots would be stripped along with Na<sup>+</sup> during the discharge process and return to the electrolyte for recycling, ultimately achieving a 1200&nbsp;h cycle life of Na||Na symmetric cells at 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with 13&nbsp;mV polarization potential and 96% capacity retention of Na||NVP full cells at 5&nbsp;C after 1800 cycles.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202413488-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202413488-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202413488-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202413488-sec-0030-title">2.1 Characterization of N,S-CDs</h3>
            
            <p>The structure and chemical composition of N,S-CDs were analyzed via a series of characterization methods. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0001">1a–c</a>, transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images with different resolutions exhibit that the synthesized N,S-CDs with a size of 3–5&nbsp;nm have spherical morphology and can be uniformly dispersed. High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images inserted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0001">1c</a> indicate the highly crystalline region within N,S-CDs. However, the X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern of N,S-CDs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information) shows a clear broad peak near 20°, indicating an amorphous carbon structure. The main reason is that carbon dots are quantum-sized, and exhibit microscopic order and macroscopic disorder.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202413488-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0d19e449-f5f6-422a-8bfb-89c4d6fe1399/adfm202413488-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0d19e449-f5f6-422a-8bfb-89c4d6fe1399/adfm202413488-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/622f710f-f12e-42da-a858-43f606e1f430/adfm202413488-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0d19e449-f5f6-422a-8bfb-89c4d6fe1399/adfm202413488-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202413488-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202413488" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a–c) TEM images of N,S-CDs at different resolutions. d) FTIR spectrum of N, S-CDs. e) XPS survey spectrum of N,S-CDs. High-resolution XPS. f) C 1s, g) N 1s, h) S 2p, i) O 1s spectra of N,S-CDs.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Furthermore, Fourier transform infrared (FT-IR) spectroscopy and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) were performed to investigate the chemical structure of N,S-CDs. The characteristic absorption bands of FT-IR result are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0001">1d</a>, the broad absorption peak near 3423&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponds to O─H and N─H groups.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0020_R_d68492806e572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Small">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peak ≈2930&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> is attributed to the C─H stretching vibration.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0020_R_d68492806e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Small">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The absorption bands ≈1704, 1671, 1384, and 1074&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> are related to the O─C═O, C═C, C─N, and C─O/S═O functional groups vibrations, respectively,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0019_R_d68492806e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">19</a>-<a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0021_R_d68492806e587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Nano ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> while small peak ≈586&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> indicates the presence of C─S group.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0022_R_d68492806e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Chin. Chem. Lett. Small">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These absorption peaks suggest the successful doping of nitrogen and sulfur elements in carbon dots. Additionally, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was conducted to analyze the valence electron structure of carbon dots. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0001">1e</a>, the full XPS spectra of N,S-CDs display peaks at 532.3, 400.2, 285.1, and 164.1&nbsp;eV, which can be attributed to O 1s, N 1s, C 1s, and S 2p, respectively. The elemental contents (atomic percentages) of nitrogen and sulfur in N,S-CDs were 1.23% and 0.86%, respectively. High-resolution XPS spectra fitting of relevant elements was performed to further analyze the possible functional groups of carbon dots. The fitted bands of the high-resolution C&nbsp;1s spectra of N,S-CDs can be well divided into five peaks, 284.8, 285.5, 286.6, 287.8, and 289.3&nbsp;eV, corresponding to C─C/C═C, C─N/C─S, C─O, C═O/C═N, and O─C═O functional groups, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0001">1f</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0019_R_d68492806e606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">19</a>, <a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0020_R_d68492806e606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Small">20</a>, <a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0022_R_d68492806e606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Chin. Chem. Lett. Small">22</a>, <a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0023_R_d68492806e606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Small">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The N 1s spectra of N,S-CDs can be well categorized into three characteristic peaks at 399.4, 400.4, and 401.3&nbsp;eV, corresponding to pyridinic N, pyrrolic N, and graphitic N, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0001">1g</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0021_R_d68492806e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Nano ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">21</a>, <a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0024_R_d68492806e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0001">1h</a>, two peaks at 163.8 and 164.9&nbsp;eV are attributed to the S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of S in the C─S bond,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0021_R_d68492806e628" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Nano ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">21</a>, <a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0025_R_d68492806e628" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Energy Environ. Sci.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> while the characteristic peaks at 168.6 and 169.6&nbsp;eV indicate the presence of sulfur-oxygen groups in N,S-CDs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0020_R_d68492806e633" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Small">20</a>, <a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0026_R_d68492806e633" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> High-resolution O 1s spectra of N,S-CDs are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0001">1i</a>, three peaks at 532.1, 532.8, and 533.8&nbsp;eV indicate the presence of C═O, S═O, and C─O groups.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0020_R_d68492806e642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Small">20</a>, <a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0023_R_d68492806e642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Small">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>UV visible spectroscopy (UV–vis) was employed to investigate the photophysical properties of N,S-CDs. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a</a> (Supporting Information), the absorption peak near 250&nbsp;nm indicates the π–π<sup>*</sup> transition of C═C bond,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0027_R_d68492806e654" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the broad peak near 340&nbsp;nm may be owing to the n-π<sup>*</sup> transition of C═O bond or nitrogen-containing functional groups.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0028_R_d68492806e662" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, a series of emission spectra with different excitation wavelengths were obtained in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2b</a> (Supporting Information). The excitation wavelength ranges from 320 to 480&nbsp;nm with an increment of 20&nbsp;nm, and the strongest emission peak of N,S-CDs located ≈475&nbsp;nm is excited by the excitation wavelength of 360&nbsp;nm. Also, the emission spectrum follows a redshift as the excitation wavelength increases and its fluorescence intensity exhibits excitation-dependent fluorescence properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0029_R_d68492806e670" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chem. Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The luminescent properties of carbon dots can be observed more intuitively in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). N,S-CDs are dispersed in the same concentration in solvents such as water, methanol, ethanol, DMF, DMSO, NMP, and THF. It can be observed that N,S-CDs can be well dispersed in polar solvents, and show fluorescence under the irradiation of UV light.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202413488-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202413488-sec-0040-title">2.2 Sodiophilic Nature of N,S-CDs</h3>
            
            <p>DFT calculations were adopted to analyze the interactions between sodium atoms and functional groups on the surface of carbon dots. To facilitate the calculation, the model of a sodium atom on graphite containing different functional groups is established. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0002">2a</a>, the binding energies of pyrrolic N, pyridinic N, and S to Na are −1.16, −1.11, and −2.23&nbsp;eV, respectively, which indicate the strong binding effect with Na. In addition, the oxygen-containing functional groups of carbon dots also have capability of absorbing Na as reported before.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0030_R_d68492806e693" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Chem. Sci.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202413488-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0fc9682e-32b7-4cb1-bc58-6832d1664d58/adfm202413488-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0fc9682e-32b7-4cb1-bc58-6832d1664d58/adfm202413488-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fbb406b1-dafb-4c5e-9631-59ea80f6e940/adfm202413488-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0fc9682e-32b7-4cb1-bc58-6832d1664d58/adfm202413488-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202413488-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202413488" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) DFT calculation models of Na binding with different functional groups on the surface of carbon dots. b) Schematic depiction of the effect of N,S-CDs for suppressing dendrite growth.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202413488-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202413488-sec-0050-title">2.3 Electrochemical Performance of N,S-CDs</h3>
            
            <p>The obtained N,S-CDs were added into the blank electrolyte (1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DIGLYME) to configure the electrolyte with different concentrations (0.1&nbsp;mg mL<sup>−1</sup>–1.0&nbsp;mg mL<sup>−1</sup>). As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information), the modified electrolyte turned yellow and the color was deepened with the increase of carbon dots concentration. Without any agglomeration and precipitation, N,S-CDs were dispersed uniformly in the blank electrolyte under sunlight and UV light irradiation. As exhibited in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), the electrolyte solution containing N,S-CDs remained unchanged after resting for 7 days.</p>
            
            <p>Na||Cu half cells were assembled to investigate the effect of the concentrations in electrolytes on the sodium metal anode. Coulombic efficiencies of Na||Cu half-cells with a series of concentrations (0.1&nbsp;mg mL<sup>−1</sup>–0.9&nbsp;mg mL<sup>−1</sup>) of N,S-CDs as electrolyte additives at a current density of 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with a cycling capacity of 1.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> were displayed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0003">3a</a>. In comparison, 0.5 and 0.6&nbsp;mg mL<sup>−1</sup> can be considered as the optimum concentration of N,S-CDs (abbreviated as 0.5N,S-CDs and 0.6N,S-CDs). Coulombic efficiency of modified electrolytes was ≈99% after stably cycling for 250 circles, whereas the blank electrolyte began to fail after 180 circles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0003">3b</a>). The initial nucleation overpotential of Na||Cu half-cells was calculated to explore the role of N,S-CDs electrolyte additives in inducing the uniform deposition of sodium metal. The first-cycle nucleation overpotential of blank electrolyte was ≈28&nbsp;mV, whereas those of 0.5 and 0.6&nbsp;mg mL<sup>−1</sup> N,S-CDs additives were reduced at 24 and 20&nbsp;mV, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0003">3c</a>). Besides, under 0.5&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>–1.0&nbsp;mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, the blank electrolyte begins to fail after 50 cycles, whereas the battery can remain stable until 100 cycles with the addition of N,S-CDs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0003">3e</a>). As exhibited in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0003">3f</a>, the nucleation overpotential decreased to 15&nbsp;mV after the addition of 0.5&nbsp;mg mL<sup>−1</sup> N,S-CDs, while the blank electrolyte exhibited 33&nbsp;mV. The reduced deposition energy barriers are favorable for achieving uniform sodium deposition. Linear scanning voltammetry (LSV) was performed to measure the electrochemical stability window of the electrolyte. As can be seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0003">3d</a>, compared to the blank electrolyte, the electrolyte had a higher oxidation potential after the addition of carbon dots, indicating that the modified electrolytes can match the high-voltage cathode better.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202413488-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/08b8e5de-47ce-419c-aba3-68da17cf0543/adfm202413488-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/08b8e5de-47ce-419c-aba3-68da17cf0543/adfm202413488-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/63b290d6-6698-404c-9336-f52422f6710d/adfm202413488-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/08b8e5de-47ce-419c-aba3-68da17cf0543/adfm202413488-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202413488-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202413488" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Coulombic efficiencies (CE) of different concentrations of N,S-CDs dissolved in electrolyte under1.0 mA cm<sup>−</sup><sup>2</sup>–1.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. CE of Na||Cu half-cells cycled under b) 1.0 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>–1.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> and e) 0.5 mA cm<sup>−</sup><sup>2</sup>–1.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. The initial nucleation overpotentials of Na||Cu half-cells cycled under c) 1.0 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>–1.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> and f) 0.5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>–1.0&nbsp;mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. d) LSV results of various electrolytes at a scanning rate of 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. SEM images of Na||Cu half-cells deposition on the copper foil in g) blank electrolyte, h) with 0.5N,S-CDs, and i) 0.6N,S-CDs under 5.0 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>–1.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Subsequently, the surface morphology of sodium metal deposition was further analyzed by Scanning Electron Microscopy (SEM). The deposition morphology of Na||Cu half cells at a current density of 5.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and a deposition capacity of 1.0&nbsp;mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> was observed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0003">3g–i</a>. Lumpy deposition of sodium was observed on the surface of the copper foil in blank electrolyte, along with holes appearing on the deposition surface. The uneven electric field at the protruding part and holes on the contact surface between the copper foil and the electrolyte further triggered the growth of dendrite. The copper foil with N,S-CDs was deposited with no obvious dendrite protrusions on the surface. A uniform and orderly layer was formed, which significantly inhibited the growth of dendrites.</p>
            
            <p>To further verify the influence of N,S-CDs on the long-term cyclin stability of sodium metal batteries, Na||Na symmetric cells were assembled and tested at a current density of 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and a cycling capacity of 1.0&nbsp;mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0004">4a</a>, the polarization potential of the Na||Na symmetric cell in blank electrolyte increases gradually after cycling for 600&nbsp;h, which indicates that the side reaction between sodium metal and electrolyte was intensified. The inhomogeneous sodium deposition would lead to the growth and accumulation of sodium dendrite, resulting in a decrease in battery cycle efficiency. However, the overpotential of symmetric cells with 0.5 and 0.6&nbsp;mg mL<sup>−1</sup> N,S-CDs were only 13&nbsp;mV and remained stable up to 1200&nbsp;h. Moreover, the overpotentials of Na||Na symmetric cells at different current densities were smaller than those in blank electrolytes and remained stable (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0004">4b</a>). The smaller driving force of the electrochemical reaction can effectively promote the uniform deposition of sodium ions.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202413488-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/348e3bae-a7cc-401d-8aa3-12e81b6515e3/adfm202413488-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/348e3bae-a7cc-401d-8aa3-12e81b6515e3/adfm202413488-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/cdc7a7e1-507d-4471-ae74-1873a8a80d28/adfm202413488-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/348e3bae-a7cc-401d-8aa3-12e81b6515e3/adfm202413488-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202413488-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202413488" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Cycling performance of Na||Na symmetric cells with and without N,S-CDs additives under 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>-1.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. b) Rate performance of Na||Na symmetric cells at different current density with and without N,S-CDs additives. Percentage of each element content for Na anode in c) blank and d) N,S-CDs electrolytes after 50 cycles. The Nyquist plots of Na||Na symmetric cells with and without N,S-CDs additives at e) 10 cycles and f) 30 cycles with g) their corresponding charge transfer resistance.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) of Na||Na symmetric cells was measured to investigate the kinetics of N,S-CDs guiding the homogeneous deposition of the sodium ions. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0004">4e–g</a>, after cycling 30 and 50 cycles at a current density of 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and a cycling capacity of 1.0&nbsp;mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, the surface film resistance (<i>R</i><sub>sf</sub>) and charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) decreased, which was attributed to the formation of stable SEI during the cycling process. More importantly, after the addition of N,S-CDs, <i>R</i><sub>sf</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> were both smaller than that in blank electrolytes, which demonstrated faster sodium ion transfer kinetics.</p>
            
            <p>After 50 cycles of Na||Na symmetric cells at a current density of 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and a cycling capacity of 1.0&nbsp;mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, Na metal displayed a rough surface with loose needle-like irregular dendrite and partially hollowed, such uneven deposition would accumulate a large amount of dead sodium (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a</a>, Supporting Information). In sharp contrast, no obvious dendrite growth was observed on the surface of the cells with N,S-CDs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6b</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6c</a>, Supporting Information), and the sodium deposition was uniform and orderly, which further proved the well-regulated effect of N,S-CDs on the sodium deposition.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202413488-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202413488-sec-0060-title">2.4 Effect of N,S-CDs</h3>
            
            <p>The transference number of sodium ions (<i>t</i><sub>Na+</sub>) was investigated by assembling Na||Na symmetric cells. After introducing N,S-CDs, the symmetric cells exhibited a transference number of 0.82, higher than that in blank electrolytes of 0.77 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). The higher transference number is beneficial to provide faster mass transfer kinetic, which allows the ion concentration to maintain a relatively stable state during the charging and discharging process, thus realizing the uniform deposition of sodium ions.</p>
            
            <p>Furthermore, the effects of various components in the electrolyte were analyzed by Raman spectroscopy (Raman). As exhibited in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a</a> (Supporting Information), Diethylene glycol dimethyl ether (DIGLYME) solvent had three sharp peaks ≈805, 826, and 850&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0031_R_d68492806e950" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Phys. Chem. A">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In a blank electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8b</a>, Supporting Information), the interaction between sodium ions dissociated from sodium salts and solvent molecules resulted in two distinct peaks ≈842 and 869&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, while the peak ≈740&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> represented the bond formed between anion PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> through fluorine atoms, corresponding to P-F stretching.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0032_R_d68492806e968" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After introducing N,S-CDs, the total integral intensity of the coordination peaks increased (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8c</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8d</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that the number of free solvent molecules in the electrolyte decreased, and more anions participated in the solvation structure, which facilitated the preferential reduction of anionic PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> to obtain fluorine-containing SEI.</p>
            
            <p>Furthermore, the mechanism of N,S-CDs on stabilizing the sodium metal anode was explored. In-depth XPS was conducted to characterize the sodium metal anode of Na||Na symmetric cells after 50 cycles at a current density of 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and a cycling capacity of 1.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. As observed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0005">5a</a>, without sputtering, C 1s spectra could be fitted with four peaks with binding energies of 284.8&nbsp;eV (C─C/C─H), 286.5&nbsp;eV (C─O), 288.7&nbsp;eV (C═O), and 289.9&nbsp;eV (O─C═O), respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0033_R_d68492806e996" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Nano Energy Energy Storage Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Three binding energy peaks at 284.8&nbsp;eV (C─C/C─H), 286.8&nbsp;eV (C─O), and 289&nbsp;eV (O─C═O) were exhibited after 3 and 6&nbsp;nm sputtering, and the peaks at 284.8, 288.4, and 289.3&nbsp;eV after further sputtering of 3&nbsp;nm were attributed to the three functional groups of C─C/C─H, C═O, and O─C═O, respectively. High-resolution C 1s spectra at 0&nbsp;nm on the surface and sputtered at 3, 6, and 9&nbsp;nm exhibited C─C/C─H (284.8&nbsp;eV), C─O (286.5&nbsp;eV), and O─C═O (289&nbsp;eV) with N,S-CDs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0005">5e</a>). The peaks of high-resolution O 1s spectra at 530.9, 531.5, 533.2, and 536&nbsp;eV corresponded to Na─O, C═O, C─O, and Na KLL, respectively (Figure <a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0005">5b,f</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0034_R_d68492806e1008" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Energy Environ. Sci. ACS Cent. Sci. Nano Energy">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Instead, as the sputtering depth increased, the results of O 1s spectra turned to be 530.6&nbsp;eV (Na═O), 531.5&nbsp;eV (C═O), and 536&nbsp;eV (Na KLL). The components such as C─O and C═O in the C 1s and O 1s spectra originated from the decomposition products of the electrolyte such as RCH<sub>2</sub>ONa and polyether.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0035_R_d68492806e1016" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Percentages of each element content for Na anode were displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0004">4c,d</a>, the content of each element of SEI in blank electrolyte varied greatly, indicating the uneven composition of SEI. Also, the element content of C and O in SEI was higher than that with N,S-CDs at various sputtering depths, indicating the higher organic component content. The presence of oxygen-containing functional groups with an affinity for Na<sup>+</sup> in the outer layer enabled the avoidance of the concentration inhomogeneity caused by the mutation of Na<sup>+</sup> coordination. However, the porous and fragile SEI with high organic content were unable to passivate the highly reactive sodium metal, which led to the continuous decomposition of electrolytes and deterioration of cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0036_R_d68492806e1029" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ACS Mater. Lett.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202413488-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/82f54fe2-fe8a-4c14-b05f-27ad234ff191/adfm202413488-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/82f54fe2-fe8a-4c14-b05f-27ad234ff191/adfm202413488-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/39a8a6a3-6123-4cc0-84da-f0cc4a4ff697/adfm202413488-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/82f54fe2-fe8a-4c14-b05f-27ad234ff191/adfm202413488-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202413488-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202413488" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">High-resolution XPS C 1s, O 1s, F 1s, S 2p, and N 1s spectra for Na anode in (a–c) blank and (d–h) N,S-CDs electrolytes after 50 cycles.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>High-resolution F 1s spectra showed the P─F bond at ≈687&nbsp;eV originated from Na<sub>x</sub>PF<sub>y</sub>O<sub>z</sub>/Na<sub>x</sub>PF<sub>y</sub> (Figure <a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0005">5c,g</a>), the decomposition product of sodium salt.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0037_R_d68492806e1069" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Energy Environ. Sci.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na─F bond at ≈684&nbsp;eV represented NaF component. After introducing N,S-CDs, a higher F content in SEI film was achieved. With the lowest electron affinity energy and excellent electron insulating ability,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0038_R_d68492806e1074" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NaF is supposed to enhance the strength and stability of SEI, consequently inhibiting the side reaction between sodium metal and solvent molecules, further suppressing the growth of interphase layers and sodium dendrite. Moreover, SEI film was detected with N and S elements after the introduction of N,S-CDs. Rapid diffusion of sodium ions in Na<sub>2</sub>S enables fast charge transfer at the electrode-electrolyte interface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0005">5d</a>). Li et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0039_R_d68492806e1085" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Mater.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> demonstrated that Na<sup>+</sup> migration had a lower activation energy barrier through Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> than NaF. Also, a minor amount of organic polyether product would reduce the charge transfer resistance and energy barrier, therefore the special structure of the inner layer of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and NaF dispersed in the polyether could improve the sodium ion conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0036_R_d68492806e1101" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ACS Mater. Lett.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> N 1s spectra with peaks at 398.5 and 403.6&nbsp;eV originated from Na<sub>3</sub>N and NaNO<sub>2</sub>, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0005">5h</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0040_R_d68492806e1114" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Both Na<sub>3</sub>N and NaN<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> are sodium ion conductors that can alleviate inhomogeneous sodium deposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0041_R_d68492806e1126" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na-N contributes to faster migration of Na<sup>+</sup> flow and also has higher mechanical strength (shear modulus: 23.95&nbsp;GPa),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202413488-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202413488-bib-0042_R_d68492806e1133" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Phys. B">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which is conducive to the retention of SEI.</p>
            
            <p>With the aim of clarifying the interaction between N,S-CDs, and SEI formation during the cycling process, the surfaces of Na||Na symmetric cells under 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>–10.0&nbsp;mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> and after 10&nbsp;h of deposition followed by 10&nbsp;h of stripping of the sodium metal anode were characterized by XPS sputtering, respectively. As exhibited in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0006">6a</a>, the high-resolution C 1s spectrum showed C─C/C─H (284.8&nbsp;eV), C─O (286.5&nbsp;eV) and O─C═O (289&nbsp;eV) components on the surface of the metal electrode and C─C/C─H (284.8&nbsp;eV), C═O (287.5&nbsp;eV), O─C═O (289.3&nbsp;eV) after 3&nbsp;nm sputtering. High-resolution N 1s spectrum was fitted with NaNO<sub>3</sub> (407.8&nbsp;eV), NaNO<sub>2</sub> (403.8&nbsp;eV), C─N (400.7&nbsp;eV), and Na<sub>3</sub>N (398.5&nbsp;eV) both on the surface and 3&nbsp;nm sputtering after deposition (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0006">6b</a>). In the S 2p spectrum, Na<sub>2</sub>S, Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>x</sub>, and Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> components were presented on the surface, and Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> also appeared after 3&nbsp;nm sputtering (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0006">6c</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202413488-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e4473df1-936b-46f1-9195-deea9607f18d/adfm202413488-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e4473df1-936b-46f1-9195-deea9607f18d/adfm202413488-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/632c1aad-252e-44a8-b531-8b9aed457e17/adfm202413488-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e4473df1-936b-46f1-9195-deea9607f18d/adfm202413488-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202413488-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202413488" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">High-resolution XPS a) C 1s, b) N 1s and c) S 2p spectra for Na anode in N,S-CDs electrolytes under 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> – 10.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. High-resolution XPS d) C 1s, e) N 1s and f) S 2p spectra for Na anode in N,S-CDs electrolytes after sodium deposition of 10&nbsp;mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> and stripping of the same amount of deposition at 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>As illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0006">6d</a>, high-resolution C 1s spectra exhibited similar organic deposition after stripping. However, only NaNO<sub>2</sub> (402.5&nbsp;eV) and Na<sub>3</sub>N (398.6&nbsp;eV) remained on the surface and Na<sub>3</sub>N (398.5&nbsp;eV) was presented after 3&nbsp;nm sputtering (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0006">6e</a>). C─N bonds existing in N,S-CDs vanished on account of the migration of carbon dots from the Na metal anode into the electrolyte during the stripping process. However, some carbon dots still remained in SEI film as active sites to induce sodium ions deposition. The tough combination of sulfur atoms and sodium ions allowed the sulfur component to remain inside the SEI during the stripping process, displaying the same S 2p spectrum regardless of deposition or exfoliation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0006">6f</a>).</p>
            
            <p>The above statement was also demonstrated by confocal fluorescence microscopy by assembling Na||Cu half-cells with 1.0, 3.0, and 5.0 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> deposition of Na on copper foil collector, respectively. As illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0007">7a-c</a>, the presence of green fluorescence signals on the collector with different amounts of sodium deposition was observed. It was noteworthy that most of the fluorescence signals appearing at the edges of the collector were on account of the correlation between the fluorescence signal and the thickness of the sodium deposition layer. Meanwhile, an obvious decrease in the fluorescence signal was observed in the cells with the same capacity of deposition followed by stripping (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202413488-fig-0007">7d-f</a>), revealing the stripping of carbon dots to the electrolyte after the discharge process. The repeated cycle ensured the constant concentration of N,S-CDs during the charge–discharge process.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202413488-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d66d63c1-e59e-4be2-ac86-eba6a711d458/adfm202413488-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d66d63c1-e59e-4be2-ac86-eba6a711d458/adfm202413488-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/25a3fdc5-d351-4a28-9af1-d0521c2d3e41/adfm202413488-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d66d63c1-e59e-4be2-ac86-eba6a711d458/adfm202413488-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202413488-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202413488" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Confocal luminescence images of the surface of copper foil at 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and sodium deposition of a1, a2) 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, b1, b2) 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, c1, c2) 5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. Confocal luminescence images of the surface of copper foil after sodium deposition of d1, d2) 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, e1, e2) 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, f1, f2) 5 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> and stripping of the same amount of deposition at 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. In Fluorescence (a1–f1) and Overlay (a2–f2).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Na||NVP full cells were assembled to evaluate the compatibility of N,S-CDs and cathode. After the activation of three cycles at 0.1 C, the specific capacity of the Na||NVP full cells decreased to 62&nbsp;mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1800 cycles at 5&nbsp;C (1&nbsp;C = 117&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>) in blank electrolyte, whereas the specific capacity was ≈80 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with N,S-CDs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a</a>, Supporting Information). The capacity decay was suppressed apparently with a capacity retention of ≈96%. As illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9b</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9c</a> (Supporting Information), the electrochemical polarization decreased after the introduction of N,S-CDs, allowing for a highly reversible capacity of the battery. As expected, excellent rate performance and low electrochemical polarization at different current densities (1.0, 5.0, 10, 15, 20, and 5.0&nbsp;C) were achieved with the assistance of N,S-CDs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, the morphology of Na metal after cycling testing was characterized. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information), large lumpy protrusions appeared on the sodium metal surface in blank electrolytes, and the disordered deposition behavior of sodium ions undoubtedly resulted in the degradation of cycling performance. However, due to the higher Na<sup>+</sup> transference number and faster transport kinetics, the Na<sup>+</sup> concentration at the electrode and electrolyte interface remained stable, thus achieving a smooth sodium ion deposition with fewer surface cracks.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202413488-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202413488-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>With excellent dispersion in electrolyte and surface polar functional groups, N,S co-doped carbon dots have been successfully synthesized. Confocal fluorescence microscopy revealed the deposition of N,S-CDs along with sodium ions on the collector and the recycling of carbon dots back into electrolytes during stripping. With the assistance of N,S-CDs, Na||Na symmetric cells maintained a long cycle life of 1200&nbsp;h at a current density of 1.0&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and a cycle capacity of 1.0&nbsp;mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. At the plating process, N,S-CDs deposited with Na<sup>+</sup> would participate in the film formation reaction, resulting in a SEI film containing organics on the surface and inorganics (NaF and Na<sub>3</sub>N) in the inner layer. The organics on the SEI surface contained a strong affinity for Na<sup>+</sup> and oxygen-containing functional groups, thus avoiding the uneven concentration caused by the mutation of Na<sup>+</sup> coordination. Superior mechanical strength and surface interface properties of NaF in the inner SEI layer boosted the strength and stability of SEI, further inhibiting side reactions between Na metal and solvent molecules. Besides, Na<sub>3</sub>N, Na<sub>2</sub>S, and NaN<sub>x</sub>O<sub>y</sub> served as favorable Na<sup>+</sup> conductors that could accelerate the migration of Na<sup>+</sup> flux and contributed to maintaining SEI, eventually forming a smooth deposition surface.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
